HPI

Chief Complaint Patient presents with ¢ Abscess

The patient is a 50-year-old female presenting transferred from Saint Joe's hospital with impression of a left peritonsillar abscess. The patient presented to the outside hospital initially with complaints of 2 days of dysphagia, odynophagia, left-sided neck pain. At the outside hospital day performed a CT neck with contrast for her to use it neck pain and swelling which demonstrated the PTA. After imaging results the outside hospital administered 1 g vancomycin, 600 mg clindamycin, Toradol and Dilaudid for pain control, and transferred the patient here for further management. On presentation here the patient continues to complain of the left-sided neck pain his dysphagia and odynophagia. She reports she has not eaten anything in 2 days with the exception of obstacles, has been trying to drink but this has been difficult to day. She did have a fluid bolus at the outside hospital. The patient otherwise denies having any nausea, vomiting, fever, chills, difficulty breathing, abdominal pain, or any other complaints at this time. She has not had any similar symptoms previously.

No data recorded Patient History

[DOB-311] - ED in PAV A Emergency Department (continued)

ED Provider Note (continued

Patient seen and examined with Dr. Bowers.

Caryn Leigh McCay is a 50 y.o. female with pcDM presenting transferred from OSH with c/o dysphagia, odynophagia, left sided neck pain, with impression of left PTA from OSH imaging. History was obtained by patient. On arrival, the patient was hemodynamically stable, non-febrile, and non-toxic appearing. Physical exam was notable for very uncomfortable appearing patient, tearful, however without any respiratory distress, trismus limits exam of tonsils but uvula appears deviated to right. Differential diagnosis includes but is not limited to strep throat, viral URI, retropharyngeal abscess, peritonsillar abscess, laryngitis, Lemierre's. Ruling out the most morbid conditions drove my assessment.

External notes independently reviewed from outside hospital and pertinent for:

- CT neck with contrast which demonstrated asymmetric enlargement of the left palatine tonsillar area with an underlying 1.8 cm focus of developing phlegmon/abscess. This displaces the airway to the right and there is mild to moderate narrowing of the airway

- medications PTA included 1g Vanc, 600mg Clindamycin, Decadron

Initial Assessment: - 50 yo F with hx DM here with left PTA, needing abscess drainage and abx

Interventions/Medications:

- fluid bolus, Dilaudid for pain control

- Promptly consulted and had an interactive discussion with ENT who came to evaluate the patient - Abscess pocket drained at bedside by ENT

- Per ENT ok for remainder of management outpatient with 7-10 days of antibiotics

- We elected for 10 days of Clindamycin instead of Augmentin given her PCN allergy

Reassessment: - after abscess drainage the patient feels much more comfortable and her symptoms have largely resolved. She is eager to be discharged home now

Impression: 1. Peritonsillar abscess

Disposition: - discharge home with outpt abx

ED Prescriptions None

1. Consult to ENT/Otolaryngology [136005851] ordered by Noel, Brennan S, MD at 08/18/23 1621 Attestation signed by Fritz, Mark A, MD at 8/19/2023 4:47 PM Signature Only

Otolaryngology—Head and Neck Surgery Consult Note 08/18/23

HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS

Caryn Leigh McCay is a 50 y.o. female with history of diabetes who presented to UK ED for concerns of left peritonsillar abscess. She reports her symptoms of left throat and neck pain along with odynophagia began 2 days ago. Her has extreme throat pain requiring multiple doses of IV opiates. She has some URI symptoms including clear drainage from nose and eyes, sneezing, but denies any trismus, difficulty breathing, fevers, n/v/d, or recent infections. She has had poor PO intake secondary to odynophagia over the last 2 days.

She has history of tonsillectomy in her childhood. She was seen at OSH where she was given vancomycin, clindamycin, toradol, IV steroids, dilaudid and a fluid bolus. She reports mild symptom improvement with the above treatments. She has diabetes and takes 50 of Levemer BID per patient. He smokes 1ppd since age 13. She has history of drug use snorting, no IV, but stopped 6 years ago.

REVIEW OF SYSTEMS A 14-point review of systems was performed and is negative except as noted in HPI.

Incision and Drainage of Abscess:

DISCUSSION OF PROCEDURE: After informed consent was obtained verbally, the patient was prepped and draped in the standard fashion for peritonsillar abscess drainage. Topical anesthesia in the form of benzocaine spray was used to anesthetize the oropharynx. Next, 1% lidocaine with 1:100,000 epinephrine was injected into the left anterior tonsillar pillar for added local anesthesia. Next, the oropharynx was palpated and the area of greatest fluctuance was identified. Using an 18-gauge spinal needle, the area of greatest fluctuance was aspirated. No purulence was removed. An 11 blade was used to make an incision in the medial most aspect of the abscess. Purulence was expressed and the patient reported foul-tasting drainage. No culture was able to be obtained. The abscess pocket was thoroughly irrigated with saline. The patient tolerated the procedure well. Dr. Fritz was available for this procedure.

ASSESSMENT/PLAN

Caryn Leigh McCay is a 50 y.o. female with histoy of diabetes who presents with concerns of small parapharyngeal abscess on left. Her symptoms have progressively worsened with mild improvement with antibiotics at OSH. Her abscess is lateral and extends inferiorly to epiglottis but no edema of glottis. Airway and subglottis widely patent on scope exam. The abscess pocket was drained at bedside with purulent drainage. Patient reported near immediate improvement in her symptoms

Recommendations:

- From ENT perspective, OK for discharge and outpatient management

- 7-10 days of PO augmentin

- Recommend against PO steroids given poorly-controlled DM

- Pain medication per ED

- Advance diet as tolerated

- Patient should follow-up with ENT (Dr. Fritz) in 3-4 weeks. We will arrange for this appt.

This Consult, Assessment, and Plan has been discussed with Dr. Frtiz, Attending Physician. Thank you for involving us in the care of this patient. Please call with any questions or concerns.

Open Wounds (No packing or drain) Open wounds should be dressed with dry gauze until fully healed. You will need to change the dressing every 24 hours. o Wash hands before and after dressing wound. Clean area with soap and water in shower.

e Vaseline may be applied to wound.

e You may soak the wound several times a day in warm, soapy water. o Replace with a fresh dressing after soaking is completed.

Incision and Drainage with Packing

e You will need to change the dressing every 24 hours. o Wash hands before and after dressing wound.

§=©Clean area with soap and water in shower.

e ~§=©Gently pull packing strip out %-1 inch and cut with scissors.

Vaseline may be applied to wound.

Apply non-stick dressing or pad and tape.

e —If there is still drainage, you may put gauze over non-stick pad. o Donot put gauze directly over wound. It will stick to the packing and possibly

pull it out at the next dressing change. e Do this once a day until packing is gone. Continue wound care after packing is out until wound is healed.

Loop Drainage

e You will need to change the dressing every 24 hours. o Wash hands before and after dressing wound.

Please keep the areas surrounding the abscess clean and dry.

Vaseline may be applied to wound.

Keep area clean and cover with gauze to absorb the residual drainage. o Drainage may persist for up to 7-10 days and is acceptable.

° Gently wiggle the loop daily to encourage drainage. §=©Clean area with soap and water in shower. e You may soak the wound several times a day in warm, soapy water.

o Replace with a fresh dressing after soaking is completed. The loop drain can removed at Urgent Care or PCP's office once: o Drainage has stopped. o Cellulitis has improved. o Usually is within 7-10 days. What everyone needs to know about AIDS Kentucky law requires that we inform you about AIDS.

Printed on 10/11/23 9:33 AM Page 45




800 Rose St C601 McCay, Caryn Leigh Lexington KY 40536-0001 MRN: 006386932, DOB: [DOB-335], Sex: F He althc are Adm: 8/18/2023, D/C: 8/18/2023

[DOB-259] - ED in PAV A Emergency Department (continued)

Documents (continued

HIV stands for "human immunodeficiency virus," which is a virus that attacks the body's immune system. HIV makes it harder for people with it to recover from other infections and illnesses. AIDS stands for "acquired immune deficiency syndrome," which is the health effects that happen if HIV is not treated. With treatment, a person living with HIV can be healthy and live nearly as long as a person who does not have HIV. Treatment for HIV also greatly lowers the chance that someone with HIV will pass it to others.

HIV is not spread through activities such as hugging or shaking hands or sharing a drinking glass. HIV is only spread through blood, sexual fluids, or breast milk.

This usually happens by:

e Having sex without condoms e Sharing needles

A mother living with HIV can pass HIV to her baby, but this is now very uncommon in the US as long as the mother knows that she has HIV and is being treated. It is also very rare that HIV is passed by blood transfusions or transplanted organs, because all donated blood and organs are now tested for HIV.

HIV is mostly spread by people who do not know they have HIV. This is why it is so important to get tested for HIV at least once in your lifetime, and at least every 6 months if you are having sex without condoms or sharing needles.

Treatment with Controlled Substances

Federal and state laws regulate controlled substances (drugs) that may be abused. Kentucky law requires that you consent to treatment with these drugs before you can receive them. Some illnesses and injuries can result in pain. Some drugs can make the pain more tolerable. Some other drugs can increase focus and reduce hyperactivity. Use of these drugs can cause nausea, sleepiness, drowsiness, vomiting, constipation, sleeplessness, loss of appetite, agitation, aggravation of depression, dry mouth, confusion, slower breathing, and loss of coordination making it unsafe to drive or operate machinery. These drugs can result in physical dependence, meaning that abrupt stopping may lead to withdrawal symptoms, psychological dependence, meaning that stopping may cause you to crave the drug, tolerance, meaning you need more drugs to get the same effect and addiction, meaning you may develop problems based on genetic or other factors. You must tell your doctor if you are pregnant or are considering pregnancy.

Living Wills

Please tell your provider if you have a Living Will, Advance Directive, Power of Attorney or any other document that allows someone to make healthcare decisions for you or documents your wishes regarding your care. You are responsible for telling your care team if you have any of these kinds of documents.

Instructions

You were evaluated in the Emergency Department for an abscess. Please keep the areas surrounding the abscess clean and dry. If the abscess progresses, you may have to have the abscess incised and drained. If your abscess was incised and drained during your visit, please follow wound care instructions attached. It is important to be aware of any new or worsening symptoms, as some problems may take longer to appear.

Follow-up with ENT as instructed.

+ We recommend you take Ibuprofen and/or Tylenol every 6 hours as needed for pain. + Alternating Tylenol and Ibuprofen every 3 hours is an excellent strategy for reducing fever or pain. ° For instance, at 12 p.m. take Ibuprofen, then at 3 p.m. take Tylenol, then at 6 p.m. take Ibuprofen.

- Please take the prescribed antibiotics for the full course of the medication as directed. Follow up with your primary care physician within 2 days for a wound check.

Return to the Emergency Department if you experience worsening pain, persistent fevers greater than 100.4, an increase in area of redness, increased tenderness/warmth around the abscess, foul smelling discharge from the abscess, or any other concerning symptoms.

Thank you for choosing us for your care.

Open Wounds (No packing or drain) + Open wounds should be dressed with dry gauze until fully healed. + You will need to change the dressing every 24 hours. e Wash hands before and after dressing wound. + Clean area with soap and water in shower. Vaseline may be applied to wound. You may soak the wound several times a day in warm, soapy water. ° Replace with a fresh dressing after soaking is completed. Continue wound care until wound is healed.

Incision and Drainage with Packing + You will need to change the dressing every 24 hours. * Wash hands before and after dressing wound. Clean area with soap and water in shower. Gently pull packing strip out 2-1 inch and cut with scissors. Vaseline may be applied to wound. Apply non-stick dressing or pad and tape. If there is still drainage, you may put gauze over non-stick pad. ° Do not put gauze directly over wound. It will stick to the packing and possibly pull it out at the next dressing change. Do this once a day until packing is gone. Continue wound care after packing is out until wound is healed.

[DOB-250] - ED in PAV A Emergency Department (continued)

Documents (continued

Attached Information Abscess, Antibiotic Treatment Only (English)

Abscess (Antibiotic Treatment Only)

An abscess happens when bacteria get trapped under the skin and start to grow. Pus forms inside the abscess as the body reacts to the bacteria. An abscess can happen with an insect bite, ingrown hair, blocked oil gland, pimple, cyst, or puncture wound. It is sometimes call a boil.

In the early stages, your wound may be red and sore. For this stage, you may get antibiotics. If the abscess doesn't get better with antibiotics, it will need to be drained with a small cut.

Home care

These tips will help you care for your abscess at home:

* Soak the wound in hot water or apply hot packs (small towel soaked in hot water) to the area for 20 minutes at a time. Do this 3 to 4 times a day, or as instructed. Use a new towel each time. Wash the towels afterward because they may be contaminated with bacteria after use.

* Don't cut, squeeze, or pop the boil yourself.

* Put antibiotic cream or ointment on the skin 3 to 4 times a day, unless something else was prescribed. Some

ointments include an antibiotic plus a pain reliever.

If your healthcare provider prescribed antibiotics, don't stop taking them until you have finished the medicine, or

you are told to stop.

* You may use an over-the-counter pain medicine to control pain, unless another pain medicine was prescribed. Talk with your provider before taking these medicines if you have chronic liver or kidney disease or ever had a stomach ulcer or digestive bleeding.

Follow-up care

Follow up with your healthcare provider, or as advised. Check your wound each day for signs that the infection may be getting worse (see below).

When to get medical advice

Call your healthcare provider right away if any of these occur:

* An increase in redness or swelling * Red streaks in the skin leading away from the abscess * An increase in local pain or swelling * Fever of 100.4°F (38°C) or higher, or as directed by your provider * Pus or fluid coming from the abscess * Boil returns after getting better StayWell last reviewed this educational content on 2/1/2022 © 2000-2023 The StayWell C

professional medical care. Al

mpany, LLC. All rights reserved. This information is not intended as a substitute for ays follow your healthcare professional's instructions.

Narrative

Narrative: DISPATCH:

EMSS was dispatched by Jessamine County E911 to 1250 KEENE RD for reports of a female patient needing transfered to UK ER for admission/surgery For peri tonsillar abscess.

RESPONSE:

EMSS initiated an non-immediate response. Responding no lights or sirens to the scene.

ARRIVAL:

Upon arrival we Found a 50 year old female. Patient is Found lying on ER stretcher/bed at emergency room. Patient is conscious and alert, AOx4 with a GCS of 15. Airway is patent with normal respirations, radial pulse noted to be strong. The patient's chief complaint is left side neck pain . Duration of chief complaint is 2 days. General impression of the patient is moderate distress. The patient was ambulatory on scene and able to Fully move with a good range of motion. The patient was secured to stretcher using stretcher straps and stretcher was secured into ambulance and patient was positioned semi-

fowlers.

ASSESSMENT:

Assessment of chief complaint includes: Left side neck pain, left ear pain, throat pain, painful

swallowing . Patient is rating their pain 5 out of 10 on the pain scale.

Upon EMS arrival Nurse Mike Orr advised that patient presented to ER this morning with complaints of left side neck pain, left ear pain, and painful swallowing. Nurse Mike Orr dvised that a CT scan revealed that patient had a peri tonsillar abscess. Nurse Mike Orr advised that patient need to be transferred to UK ER for admission and surgery to drain the abscess. Patient was found lying in bed in ER. Patient was alert and oriented x4 with a patent airway. Nurse Mike Orr noted that patient's Vancomycin had finished running. Nurse Mike Orr then disconnected the Vancomycin and then started the Clindamycin drip.

Caryn Leigh McCay is a 50 y.o. female with PMH of diabetes who presents to the emergency department from scene for evaluation as a trauma due to a MVC. Patient was a(n) restrained driver without ejection. She reports driving at approximately 45 miles per hour when another car pulled out in front of her, causing her to hit a brick wall. The patient believes she may have lost consciousness for a brief period during the accident. She describes the impact as forceful, with airbags deploying.

Currently endorsing pain in chest, cervical, and thoracic spine without associated weakness, numbness, and tingling. Denies any recent fevers, night sweats, chills, shortness of breath, chest pain, nausea/vomiting, cough, diarrhea, or paresthesias.

Turner, David G, MD

Van Wyk, Charl S, MD

Performed: 07/30/23 2319 - 07/30/23 2337 Resulting lab: IMAGING

Narrative:

CLINICAL INDICATION:

poly trauma

Accession number: 15555557

TECHNIQUE:

Imaging of the chest abdomen and pelvis was performed, from thoracic inlet through pubic symphysis, using spiral technique, following administration of IV contrast, Omnipaque 350, 100 mL according to the CTA thoracic aorta/chest and CTA Abdomen/Pelvis protocol. Reformatted images in the coronal, sagittal, and oblique planes were generated from the axial data set to facilitate diagnostic accuracy. In addition, 3D images were created and reviewed.

Narrative:

CLINICAL INDICATION:

poly trauma

TECHNIQUE:

Imaging of the chest abdomen and pelvis was performed, from thoracic inlet through pubic symphysis, using spiral technique, following administration of IV contrast, Omnipaque 350, 100 mL according to the CTA thoracic aorta/chest and CTA Abdomen/Pelvis protocol. Reformatted images in the coronal, sagittal, and oblique planes were generated from the axial data set to facilitate diagnostic accuracy. In addition, 3D images were created and reviewed.

Multiple axial CT images were obtained through level of pelvis per CT Bony Pelvis protocol. The axial CT data set was used to

generate high resolution reformatted images in the coronal and sagittal planes to facilitate diagnostic accuracy and treatment planning.

Total DLP (Dose-Length Product): 4958 mGy-cm. Please note: The reported value represents the total of one or more individual components during the CT acquisition on this date and at this time, and as such, the same value may appear in more than one CT report depending on the interpreting/reporting physicians.

COMPARISON: CTA chest abdomen pelvis 4/16/2016

FINDINGS: Chest:

Lower neck: The supraglottic soft tissues appear circumferentially thickened.

Aorta/Vessels: No acute thoracic aortic pathology. No periaortic hematoma. No filling defect within the pulmonary arteries to suggest pulmonary embolism.

Pleural/Pericardial Space: No pneumothorax. No pleural effusions. No pericardial effusion.

AUTHORIZATIONS & AGREEMENTS . (Patient Label Here)

A mother living with HIV can pass HIV to her baby, but this is now very uncommon in the US as long as the mother knows that she has HIV and is being treated. It is also very rare that HIV is passed by blood transfusions or transplanted organs, because all donated blood and organs are now tested for HIV.

HIV is mostly spread by people who do not know they have HIV. This is why it is so important to get tested for HIV at least once in your lifetime, and at least every 6 months if you are having sex without condoms or sharing needles.

Treatment with Controlled Substances

Federal and state laws regulate controlled substances (drugs) that may be abused. Kentucky law requires that you consent to treatment with these drugs before you can receive them. Some illnesses and injuries can result in pain. Some drugs can make the pain more tolerable. Some other drugs can increase focus and reduce hyperactivity. Use of these drugs can cause nausea, sleepiness, drowsiness, vomiting, constipation, sleeplessness, loss of appetite, agitation, aggravation of depression, dry mouth, confusion, slower breathing, and loss of coordination making it unsafe to drive or operate machinery. These drugs can result in physical dependence, meaning that abrupt stopping may lead to withdrawal symptoms, psychological dependence, meaning that stopping may cause you to crave the drug, tolerance, meaning you need more drugs to get the same effect and addiction, meaning you may develop problems based on genetic or other factors. You must tell your doctor if you are pregnant or are considering pregnancy.

Living Wills

Please tell your provider if you have a Living Will, Advance Directive, Power of Attorney or any other document that allows someone to make healthcare decisions for you or documents your wishes regarding your care. You are responsible for telling your care team if you have any of these kinds of documents.

What everyone needs to know about AIDS

Kentucky law requires that we inform you about AIDS.

HIV stands for "human immunodeficiency virus," which is a virus that attacks the body's immune system. HIV makes it harder for people with it to recover from other infections and illnesses. AIDS stands for "acquired immune deficiency syndrome," which is the health effects that happen if HIV is not treated. With treatment, a person living with HIV can be healthy and live nearly as long as a person who does not have HIV. Treatment for HIV also greatly lowers the chance that someone with HIV will pass it to others.

HIV is not spread through activities such as hugging or shaking hands or sharing a drinking glass. HIV is only spread through blood, sexual fluids or breast milk.

This usually happens by: ® Having sex without condoms * Sharing needles

Additional Information

Preventing Falls in the Home

As you get older, falls are more likely. That's because your reaction time slows. Your muscles and joints may also get stiffer, making them less flexible. Illness, medications, and vision changes can also affect your balance. A fall could leave you unable to live on your own. To make your home safer, follow these tips:

Moving:

* Get up slowly after you sit or lie down. + Wear shoes inside and outside the house. Do not go barefoot or wear slippers. Floors:

Put nonskid pads under area rugs.

Remove throw rugs. Or hold the rugs in place with 2-sided tape or nonslip backing.

Replace worn floor coverings.

Tack carpets firmly to each step on carpeted stairs. Put nonskid strips on the edges of uncarpeted stairs. Keep floors and stairs free of clutter and cords.

Arrange furniture so there are clear pathways.

Clean up any spills right away.

Stairs:

+ Always use the handrails. There should be handrails on both sides. The handrails should be as long as the stairs. + Use a contrasting color on the top edge of all steps. This will help you see the steps better.

Be very careful if you miss or change doses, feel sick, or start a new medicine. Let your doctor know right away if you feel very sleepy and can't stay awake for normal activities.

Why do I need a naloxone prescription?

Naloxone is a rescue medicine. It is only used to treat an opioid overdose. Most people who are prescribed an opioid do not have any problems. But even if you are careful, there is still a risk of accidental overdose.

Make sure someone close to you knows the signs of overdose and how to give naloxone.

Most opioid deaths are accidents

Opioid overdose deaths are happening more often in the U.S. In fact, a person dies from opioid overdose every 11 minutes,

+ In 2017, opioid overdose caused more than 47,000 deaths. + Most of those deaths were accidents. + Nearly 4 out of 10 people who die from opioid overdose have an opioid prescription.

Death from overdose is more common than you think.

Attached Information MVA, No Serious Injury (English)

Motor Vehicle Accident: No Serious Injury

You or your child have been seen today because of a car accident. Your exam does not show any sign of serious injury from your car accident. It's important to watch for any new symptoms that might be a sign of hidden injury.

It can be normal to feel sore and tight in your muscles and back the next day, and not just the muscles you initially injured. Remember, all the parts of your body are connected, so while initially one area hurts, the next day another may hurt. Injuries cause inflammation, which then causes the muscles to tighten up and hurt more. After the initial worsening, it should slowly improve over the next few days. However, report more severe pain to your healthcare provider.

Even without a definite head injury, you can still get a concussion from your head suddenly jerking forward, backward, or sideways. It's common to have a mild headache and feel tired, nauseated, or dizzy. Concussions and even bleeding can still occur, especially if you've had a recent injury, take blood thinners, or are over age 65. Know the warning signs that you should report to your healthcare provider.

Even without physical injury, a car accident can be very stressful. It can cause emotional or mental symptoms after the event. These may include:

* General sense of anxiety and fear

* Recurring thoughts or nightmares about the accident

* Trouble sleeping or changes in appetite

* Feeling depressed, sad, or low in energy

* Being irritable or easily upset

+ Feeling the need to avoid activities, places, or people that remind you of the accident In most cases, these are normal reactions. And they're not severe enough to interfere with your normal activities. They should go away in a few days or a few weeks. Talk with your healthcare provider if these reactions last longer, get worse, or disrupt your daily life.

Home care Muscle pain, sprains and strains

Even if you have no visible injury, it's common to be sore all over, and have new aches and pains the first couple of days after an accident. Take it easy at first, and don't overdo it.

+ At first, don't try to stretch out the sore spots. If there is a strain, stretching may make it worse.

* You can use an ice pack or cold compress on the sore spots for up to 20 minutes at a time, as often as you feel comfortable. This may help reduce the inflammation, swelling, and pain. To make an ice pack, put ice cubes ina plastic bag that seals at the top. Wrap the bag in a thin towel or cloth. Don't put the ice pack directly on the skin.

+ Sometimes, after the pain and inflammation heal you can be left with a good amount of stiffness. In this case, you can use a heating pad, especially on your low back.

Wound care

* If you have any scrapes or abrasions, they often heal in about10 days. It's important to keep the abrasions clean while they first start to heal. Follow wound care instructions from your healthcare provider. Watch for early signs of infection such as:

° Increasing redness, warmth, or swelling around the wound © Fever ° Red streaking lines around the wound

* Draining pus

Medicines

* Talk to your healthcare provider before taking new medicine, especially if you have other medical problems or are taking other medicines. If you need anything for pain, you can take acetaminophen or ibuprofen, unless you were given a different pain medicine to use. Ibuprofen is an anti-inflammatory agent and helps more with muscle soreness. Talk with your provider before using these medicines if you have medicine allergies, chronic liver or kidney disease, stomach ulcer or gastrointestinal bleeding, or are taking blood thinner medicines. Always follow your provider's instructions. Be careful if you're given prescription pain medicines, narcotics, or medicines for muscle spasm. They can make you sleepy, dizzy and can affect your coordination, reflexes and judgment. Don't drive or do work where you can injure yourself when taking them.

Follow-up care

Follow up with your healthcare provider, or as advised. If emotional or mental symptoms persist or get worse, follow up with your provider right away. You may have a more serious traumatic stress reaction. There are treatments that can help.

If X-rays or a CT scan were done, you'll be told if there is a change that affects treatment. Call 911

Call 911 if any of these occur:

* Trouble breathing

* One pupil is larger than the other

* Repeated vomiting

* Headache that worsens and does not go away

* Restlessness or agitation

* Confusion, drowsiness, or trouble arousing

+ Fainting, loss of consciousness, convulsions, or seizures * Rapid heart rate

* Trouble with speech or sight

* Trouble walking, loss of balance, numbness or weakness in 1 side of your body, facial droop When to get medical advice

Call your healthcare provider right away if any of the following occur:

* New or worsening pain in neck, back, belly, arm, or leg

* Redness, swelling, or pus coming from any wound

* Mental and emotional symptoms that don't get better or that get worse StayWell last reviewed this educational content on 1/1/2022 © 200¢
